<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox vevent"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above above" style="font-size:125%; color: black; background-color: salmon;">Wu</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Region</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, Southeastern <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> provinces</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Ethnicity</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese-speaking_people" title="Wu Chinese-speaking people">Wu Chinese people</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; <ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:1.35em;padding-left:0"><li>
<a href="/wiki/Sinitic_languages" title="Sinitic languages">Sinitic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><b>Wu</b></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr class="plainlist"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Dialects</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;">
<ul><li>5-6 major groups. Notable localities include <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhounese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Wenzhou dialect">Wenzhounese</a></li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">Writing system</a></span></div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="color: black; background-color: salmon;">Language codes</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-3" title="ISO 639-3">ISO 639-3</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/wuu" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:wuu">wuu</a></code></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></i></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/wuch1236">wuch1236</a></code></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">Linguasphere</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code>79-AAA-d</code></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Idioma_wu.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Idioma_wu.png/320px-Idioma_wu.png" decoding="async" width="320" height="279" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/Idioma_wu.png 1.5x" data-file-width="362" data-file-height="316" /></a></span><div style="text-align:left;"><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This image needs references to reliable sources (November 2022)">image reference needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Wu</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">吳語</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">吴语</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">Wu romanization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese#IPA" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">IPA</a>: <span title="Wu Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="wuu-Latn">wu<sup>6</sup> gniu<sup>6</sup></i></span> [<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɦu˩˩˧.n̠ʲy˩˩˧</span>] (<a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>), <span title="Wu Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="wuu-Latn">ghou<sup>2</sup> gniu<sup>6</sup></i></span> [<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɦou˨˨˦.n̠ʲy˨˧˩</span>] (<a href="/wiki/Suzhounese" class="mw-redirect" title="Suzhounese">Suzhounese</a>), Mandarin <span title="Mandarin Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="cmn-Latn">Wúyǔ</i></span> [<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">u³⁵<span class="wrap"> </span>y²¹⁴</span>]) is a major group of <a href="/wiki/Sinitic_languages" title="Sinitic languages">Sinitic languages</a> spoken primarily in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang Province</a>, and the part of <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu Province</a> south of the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a>, which makes up the cultural region of <a href="/wiki/Wu_(region)" title="Wu (region)">Wu</a>. Speakers of various Wu languages sometimes labelled their mother tongue as <b>Shanghainese</b> when introduced to foreigners. The <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhou dialect</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestige dialect">prestige dialect</a> of Wu as of the 19th century, but had been replaced in status by <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a> by the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Shanghai" title="History of Shanghai">turn of the 20th century</a>. The languages of <a href="#subdivision">Northern Wu</a> are <a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a> with each other, while those of <a href="#subdivision">Southern Wu</a> are not.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">Historical linguists</a> view Wu of great significance because it distinguished  itself from other <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a> by preserving the <a href="/wiki/Voiced_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced consonant">voiced initials</a> of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> and by preserving the <a href="/wiki/Checked_tone" title="Checked tone">checked tone</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988180_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988180-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The phonological divergence between Wu and other Chinese is significant, for instance, the phrase "Shanghainese language" (<span lang="zh-Hant">上海閒話</span>; <span lang="zh-Hans">上海闲话</span>) is pronounced as [<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">zɑ̃.he̞<span class="wrap"> </span>ɦe̞.ɦo</a></span>] in Shanghainese and <span class="IPA" lang="cmn-Latn-fonipa" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[ʂaŋ.xaɪ.ɕjɛn.xwa]</a></span> in Beijing Mandarin. Wu and the Suzhou dialect in particular is perceived as soft in the ears of Mandarin speakers; hence there is the idiom "the tender speech of Wu" (<span lang="zh-Hant">吳儂軟語</span>; <span lang="zh-Hans">吴侬软语</span>).
</p><p>The decline of Wu began from around 1986, when students were banned from speaking "uncivilized dialects" during class, a term used by the <a href="/w/index.php?title=State_Language_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="State Language Commission (page does not exist)">State Language Commission</a> to refer to <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">all Chinese languages</a> other than <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1992, students in Shanghai were banned from speaking Wu at all times on campuses.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Since the late 2000s, Wu mostly survived in kitchens and theatres, as a "kitchen language" among the elderly housewives and as a theatrical language in folk Yue opera, Shanghai opera and Pingtan. As of now, Wu has no official status, no legal protection and there is no officially sanctioned <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">romanization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Xinmin_Weekly_2012_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Xinmin_Weekly_2012-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">Varieties of Chinese</a></div>
<p>Speakers of Wu varieties are mostly unaware of this term for their speech, since the term "Wu" is a relatively recent classificatory imposition on what are less clearly defined and highly heterogeneous natural forms. Saying someone "speaks Wu" is therefore akin to saying someone "speaks a Romance language"; it is not a particularly defined entity like <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Mandarin">Standard Mandarin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Hochdeutsch</a>.
</p><p>Most speakers are only vaguely aware of their local variety's affinities with other similarly classified varieties, and will generally only refer to their local Wu variety rather than to the dialect family as a whole. This is typically done by affixing <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">話</span></span> ('speech') to a location's endonym. For example, <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">溫州話</span></span> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">Wu Chinese pronunciation:</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="wuu-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Wu_Chinese" title="Help:IPA/Wu Chinese">&#91;ʔy˧꜖<span class="wrap"> </span>tɕiɤu˧꜖<span class="wrap"> </span>ɦo˩꜒꜔&#93;</a></span>) is used for <a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Wenzhounese</a>. Affixing <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">閒話</span></span>' is also common, and more typical of the Taihu division, as in <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">嘉興閒話</span></span> (<span title="Wu Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="wuu-Latn">ka-shin ghae-o</i></span>) for the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jiaxing_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jiaxing dialect (page does not exist)">Jiaxing dialect</a>.
</p>
<dl><dt>Wu</dt></dl>
<p><span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">吳語</span></span></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Wúyǔ</span></i>; Shanghainese <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">&#91;ɦu˨<span class="wrap"> </span>ɲy˦&#93;</a></span>; Suzhounese <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">&#91;ɦoʊ˨<span class="wrap"> </span>ɲy˦&#93;</a></span>; Wuxinese <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">&#91;ŋ˨˨˧<span class="wrap"> </span>nʲy˨&#93;</a></span>; 'Wu language'
</p><p>The formal name and standard reference in <a href="/wiki/Dialectology" title="Dialectology">dialectology</a> literature.
</p>
<dl><dt>Wuyue language</dt></dl>
<p><span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">吳越語</span></span></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Wúyuèyǔ</span></i>; &#39;the language of Wu–Yue&#39;
</p><p>A poetic and historical name, highlighting the roots of the language in antiquity, specifically the culture of the <a href="/wiki/Wu_(state)" title="Wu (state)">Wu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yue_(state)" title="Yue (state)">Yue</a> states during the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Goetian</b>, derived from the Japanese spelling of <i>Wuyue</i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">吳越</span></span>; <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Gō-etsu</i></span>), is among the alternative names listed by <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Wu <a href="/wiki/Topolect" class="mw-redirect" title="Topolect">topolect</a></dt></dl>
<p><span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">吳方言</span></span></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Wú fāngyán</span></i>
</p><p>A derogatory and self-deprecating<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Category:Articles_contradicting_other_articles" title="Category:Articles contradicting other articles"><span title="This text contradicts material elsewhere on this page. (October 2023)">contradictory</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> yet common name that belittles the status of Wu languages as a "dialect" of a singular "Chinese language", usually used by academics in state-administered universities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Jiang–Zhe speech</dt></dl>
<p><span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">江浙話</span></span></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Jiāngzhè huà</span></i>
</p><p>A non-standard name meaning 'the speech of <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>', occasionally used to highlight the fact that the language is spoken across two provinces.
</p>
<dl><dt>Jiangnan speech</dt></dl>
<p><span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">江南話</span></span></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Jiāngnán huà</span></i>
</p><p>A non-standard, uncommon name linking the language to the cultural region of <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a>. This is not to be confused with the Jiangnan Industrial Groups Koiné spoken in <a href="/wiki/Xiangtan" title="Xiangtan">Xiangtan</a>, which is classified as a variety of Mandarin.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Wu Chinese is the most ancient of the six major southern Chinese varieties, tracing its origin to more than 3,000 years ago, when the <a href="/wiki/Predynastic_Zhou" title="Predynastic Zhou">Zhou</a> princes <a href="/wiki/Taibo" title="Taibo">Taibo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhongyong_of_Wu" title="Zhongyong of Wu">Zhongyong</a> migrated from the <a href="/wiki/Guanzhong" title="Guanzhong">Guanzhong</a> region in modern <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Wuxi" title="Wuxi">Wuxi</a>–<a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a> area of the <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a> region, where they established the state of <a href="/wiki/Wu_(state)" title="Wu (state)">Wu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51&#93;_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51]-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The northern language they brought formed the foundation of Wu Chinese. By the <a href="/wiki/Six_Dynasties" title="Six Dynasties">Six Dynasties</a> era, Wu had already been developing for a millennium and differed considerably from the northern speech. When large numbers of northern Chinese migrated to Jiangnan following the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Jin" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Jin">Western Jin</a> dynasty, they discovered great discrepancies between the two varieties of Chinese. This is recorded in contemporary texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Shi_Shuo_Xin_Yu" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi Shuo Xin Yu">Shi Shuo Xin Yu</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51&#93;_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51]-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese <a href="/wiki/Go-on" title="Go-on">Go-on</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">呉音</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">goon</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: Wú yīn)</span> readings of Chinese characters (obtained from the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Wu" title="Eastern Wu">Eastern Wu</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period) are from the ancient Wu Chinese of this period. However, as Wu Chinese has been under strong influence from the north throughout history, many of its ancient features have been lost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51&#93;_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51]-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The language of today is largely descendant from the Middle Chinese of the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a>–<a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> era (6–8th centuries), as is true of most contemporary Chinese languages, with <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min Chinese</a> languages being notable exceptions.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> However, many of the ancient Wu features have been preserved in Min,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA52_52&#93;_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA52_52]-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> as the latter began its life as the Old Wu spoken by migrants to <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> during the century that marked the transition from the late <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Jin" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Jin">Western Jin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA53_53&#93;_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA53_53]-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Substrate_influences">Substrate influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Substrate influences"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wu is considered the most ancient southern Chinese variety,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51&#93;_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhouYou2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksido142DwAAQBAJpgPA51_51]-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> since the <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a> region was the first area settled that was non-contiguous with the northern Chinese states. <a href="/wiki/Proto-Min_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Min language">Proto-Min or Old Wu–Min</a> is also the language from which the Min dialects evolved as the populace migrated farther south, so some knowledge of this language would not only offer insight into the development of these dialects and Sino-Tibetan but also into the indigenous languages of the region, knowledge of which would also be invaluable towards establishing the phylogeny of related Asian languages and towards reconstructing them.
</p><p>According to traditional history, <a href="/wiki/Taibo_of_Wu" class="mw-redirect" title="Taibo of Wu">Taibo of Wu</a> settled in the area during the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, bringing along a large section of the population and Chinese administrative practices to form the state of Wu.<sup id="cite_ref-Yuan_Jiahua_hyfygy_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yuan_Jiahua_hyfygy-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The state of Wu might have been ruled by a Chinese minority along with sinified <a href="/wiki/Yue_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Yue peoples">Yue peoples</a>, and the bulk of the population would have remained Yue until later migrations and absorption into the greater Chinese populace (though many likely fled south as well). Many have wondered about what effect the Yue people's language may have had on the dialect spoken there, since, for example, names and other social practices in the state of Yue are markedly different from the rest of Chinese civilization.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Karlgren" title="Bernhard Karlgren">Bernhard Karlgren</a>, on the other hand, noted that the Tang koiné was adopted by most speakers in China (except for those in Fujian) with only slight remnants of "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vulgar" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:vulgar">vulgar</a>" speech from pre-Tang times, which he believed were preserved among the lower classes,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> albeit this makes many presumptions about Tang China's class structure and sociolinguistic situation. Most linguists today refer to these remnants as dialectal strata or substrata. In many ways, the koiné can be considered the language from which Wu varieties evolved, with the earlier language leaving behind a pre-Tang dialectal stratum which itself may have included a substratum from the Yue languages.
</p><p>Western dialectologists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2023)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> have found a small handful of words that appear to be part of an <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Austroasiatic">Austroasiatic</a> substratum in Wu and Min languages. Mandarin Chinese also possesses some words of Austroasiatic origin<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="How is it of Austroasiatic origin? Old Chinese is older than old Vietnamese so what is the explanation to the Austroasiatic origin? (April 2023)">how?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, such as the original name of the Yangtze River, <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">江</span></span> (<span title="Mandarin Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="cmn-Latn">jiāng</i></span>; Old Chinese <span title="Old Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="och-Latn">*krung</i></span>, cf. Old Vietnamese <span title="Vietnamese-language romanization"><i lang="vi-Latn">*krong</i></span>), which has evolved into the general word for 'river'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198818_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198818-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Min languages, which were less affected by the koiné, appear to possess an Austroasiatic substratum, such as a Min word for 'shaman' or 'spirit healer', such as in Jian'ou Min  <span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="nan-Latn">toŋ³</i></span> which appears to be cognate with Vietnamese <span title="Vietnamese-language romanization"><i lang="vi-Latn">ʔdoŋ²</i></span>, Mon <span title="Mon-language text"><i lang="mnw">doŋ</i></span>, and Santali <span title="Santali-language romanization"><i lang="sat-Latn">dōŋ</i></span>, which all have meanings similar to the Min word.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198818–19_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198818–19-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> However, Laurent Sagart points out that the resemblance between the Min word for 'shaman' or 'spirit healer' and the Vietnamese term with the same meaning is likely coincidental.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagart2008142_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagart2008142-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most notable examples are the word for "person" in some Wu varieties as <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">*nong</i></span>, usually written as <span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%84%82#Traditional_Chinese" class="extiw" title="wikt:儂">儂</a></span> <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">nóng</i></span> in Chinese, and the word for 'wet' in many Wu and Min dialects, with a <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> initial which seemingly shows no relation to the Chinese word <span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%BF%95#Traditional_Chinese" class="extiw" title="wikt:濕">濕</a></span> <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">shī</i></span> but cognate with Vietnamese <span lang="vi"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C4%91%E1%BA%A7m#Vietnamese" class="extiw" title="wikt:đầm">đầm</a></span>. Min languages notably retain the bilabial nasal coda for this word. However, Sagart shows that the Min words for 'wet', 'duckweed', and 'small salted fish', which seem to be cognates with Vietnamese <span lang="vi"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C4%91%E1%BA%A7m#Vietnamese" class="extiw" title="wikt:đầm">đầm</a></span>, <span lang="vi"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/b%C3%A8o#Vietnamese" class="extiw" title="wikt:bèo">bèo</a></span>, <span lang="vi"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/k%C3%A8#Vietnamese" class="extiw" title="wikt:kè">kè</a></span>, are either East Asian areal words if not Chinese words in disguise ('duckweed', 'wet'), and long shots ('salted fish').<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESagart2008143_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESagart2008143-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Hui identifies 126 <a href="/wiki/Tai-Kadai_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Tai-Kadai languages">Tai-Kadai</a> cognates in <a href="/wiki/Minhang_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Minhang District">Maqiao</a> Wu dialect spoken in the suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> out of more than a thousand lexical items surveyed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi200115_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi200115-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> According to the author, these cognates are likely traces of "<a href="/wiki/Old_Yue_language" title="Old Yue language">old Yue language</a>" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">古越語</span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi200115_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi200115-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Analysis of the <i><a href="/wiki/Song_of_the_Yue_Boatman" title="Song of the Yue Boatman">Song of the Yue Boatman</a></i>, a song in the Yue language transcribed by a Chinese official in Chinese characters, clearly points to a Tai language rather than an Austroasiatic one.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Chinese discussion of Wenzhounese often mentions the Tai affinities the dialect possesses.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_languages" title="Zhuang languages">Zhuang languages</a> in Guangxi and western Guangdong, for example, are also Tai, so it would appear that Tai populated southern China before the Chinese expansion. The term Yue was applied indiscriminately to any non-Chinese in the area that the Chinese encountered. The impact of these languages still appears to be fairly minimal overall.
</p><p>Though Sino-Tibetan, Tai–Kadai, Austronesian and Austroasiatic are mostly considered to be unrelated to each other, Sagart has proposed some possible phylogenetic affinities. Specifically, Tai–Kadai and Sino-Tibetan could possibly both belong to the Sino-Austronesian language family (not to be confused with Austroasiatic) due to a scattering of cognates between their ancestral forms, and there is also some, albeit much more tenuous, evidence to suggest that Austroasiatic should also be included, however his views are but one among competing hypotheses about the phylogeny of these languages, see the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Austronesian_languages" title="Sino-Austronesian languages">Sino-Austronesian languages</a> article for some further detail.
</p><p>It does appear that Wu varieties have had non-Sinitic influences, and many contain words cognate with those of other languages in various strata. These words however are few and far between, and Wu on the whole is most strongly influenced by Tang Chinese rather than any other linguistic influence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migrations">Migrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Migrations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As early as the time of <a href="/wiki/Guo_Pu" title="Guo Pu">Guo Pu</a> (276–324), speakers easily perceived differences between dialects in different parts of China including the area where Wu varieties are spoken today.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin198325_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin198325-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to records of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Jin" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Jin">Eastern Jin</a>, the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_dialect" title="Nanjing dialect">dialect of Nanjing</a> was an ancient Wu dialect. After the <a href="/wiki/Wu_Hu_uprising" class="mw-redirect" title="Wu Hu uprising">Wu Hu uprising</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Disaster_of_Yongjia" title="Disaster of Yongjia">Disaster of Yongjia</a> in 311, the Jin Emperor and many northern Chinese fled south, establishing the new capital <a href="/wiki/Jiankang" title="Jiankang">Jiankang</a> in what is modern-day Nanjing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska2010161_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska2010161-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
The lower Yangtze region became heavily inundated by settlers from Northern China, mostly coming from what is now northern Jiangsu province and Shandong province, with smaller numbers of settlers coming from the Central Plains. From the 4th to the 5th century, Northern people moved into Wu areas, adding characteristics to the lexicon of Northern Wu, traces of which can still be found in Northern Wu varieties today.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2002530–531_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2002530–531-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One prominent historical speaker of the Wu dialect was Emperor <a href="/wiki/Yangdi" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangdi">Yangdi</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Empress_Xiao_(Sui_dynasty)" title="Empress Xiao (Sui dynasty)">Empress Xiao</a>. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xuan_of_Western_Liang" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Xuan of Western Liang">Emperor Xuan of Western Liang</a>, a member of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Liang" title="Emperor Wu of Liang">Emperor Wu of Liang</a>'s court, was Empress Xiao's grandfather and he most likely learned the Wu dialect at <a href="/wiki/Jiankang" title="Jiankang">Jiankang</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, in which the Wu-speaking region was devastated by war, Shanghai was inundated with migrants from other parts of the Wu-speaking area. This greatly affected the variety of Shanghai, bringing, for example, influence from the <a href="/wiki/Ningbo_dialect" title="Ningbo dialect">Ningbo dialect</a> to a dialect which, at least within the <a href="/wiki/Old_City_of_Shanghai" class="mw-redirect" title="Old City of Shanghai">walled city of Shanghai</a>, was almost identical to the <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhou dialect</a>. As a result of the population boom, in the first half of the 20th century, Shanghainese became almost a lingua franca within the region, eclipsing the status of the Suzhou variety. However, due to its pastiche of features from different languages, it is rarely used to infer historical information about the Wu group and is less representative of Wu than the Suzhou variety.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Written_sources">Written sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Written sources"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are few written sources of study for Wu, and research is generally concentrated on modern speech forms rather than texts. Written Chinese has always been in the classical form, so Wu speakers would have written in this classical form and read it in a literary form of their dialect based on the phonetic distinctions outlined in rhyme dictionaries. Therefore, no text in classical Chinese from the region would give a clear notion about the actual speech of the writer, although there may have been cleverly disguised puns based on local pronunciations that are lost on modern readers or other dialect speakers. <a href="/wiki/Yue_opera" title="Yue opera">Yue opera</a>, for example, is performed in the <a href="/wiki/Shaoxing_dialect" title="Shaoxing dialect">Shaoxing dialect</a>, however the register is more literary than oral.
</p><p>There are still a number of primary documents available, but they do not always give a clear sense of the dialects' historical pronunciation. They do often offer insight into lexical differences. Most of the sources for diachronic Wu study lie in the folk literature of the region. Since the average person was illiterate and the literate were often traditionalists who possibly perceived their local form of Chinese as a degenerated version of a classical ideal, very little was recorded, although local vocabulary often sneaks into written records.
</p><p>A "ballad–narrative" (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">說晿詞話</span></span>) known as "The Story of Xue Rengui Crossing the Sea and Pacifying Liao" (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">薛仁貴跨海征遼故事</span></span>), which is about the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> hero <a href="/wiki/Xue_Rengui" title="Xue Rengui">Xue Rengui</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> is believed to have been written in the <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhou dialect</a> of Wu.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The main sources of study are from the Ming and Qing period, since the dialectal differences were not as obvious until Ming times,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and lie in historical folk songs, <i><a href="/wiki/Tanci" title="Tanci">tanci</a></i> (a kind of ballad or lyric poem), local records, legendary stories, <a href="/wiki/Written_vernacular_Chinese" title="Written vernacular Chinese">baihua</a> novels, educational material produced for the region, notes which have survived among individuals' effects, the linguistic descriptions made by foreigners (primarily by missionaries), and the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bibles</a> translated into Wu dialects. These all give glimpses into the past, but except for the bibles, are not so useful for phonological studies. They are, however, of tremendous importance for diachronic studies of vocabulary and to a lesser extent grammar and syntax.
</p><p>The diachronic study of written Ming and Qing Wu, the time when the dialects began to take on wholly unique features, can be placed into three stages: the Early Period, the Middle Period, and the Late Period.
</p><p>The "Early Period" begins at the end of the Ming dynasty to the beginning of the Qing in the 17th century, when the first documents showing distinctly Wu characteristics appear. The representative work from this period is the collection of folk songs gathered by Feng Menglong entitled "Shan Ge" <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">山歌</span></span>. The majority of early period documents record the Wu varieties of southern Jiangsu and northern Zhejiang, so any discussion in this section is primarily relevant to Northern Wu or the Taihu division. Along with some other legends and works, the following list contains many of the documents that are either written in Wu or contain parts where dialects are used.
</p>
<ul><li><i>San Yan</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">三言</span></span>, a trilogy of collected stories compiled by <a href="/wiki/Feng_Menglong" title="Feng Menglong">Feng Menglong</a></li>
<li><i>Er Pai</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">二拍</span></span>, two short story collections by <a href="/wiki/Ling_Mengchu" title="Ling Mengchu">Ling Mengchu</a></li>
<li><i>Xing Shi Yan</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">型世言</span></span>, a novella recorded by Lu Renlong <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">陸人龍</span></span></li>
<li><i>Huan Sha Ji</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">浣紗記</span></span>, an opera by Liang Chenyu <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">梁辰魚</span></span></li>
<li><i>Mo Hanzhai dingben chuanqi</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">墨憨齋定本傳奇</span></span>, Feng Menglong</li>
<li><i>Qing zhong pu</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">清忠譜</span></span></li>
<li><i>Doupeng xianhua</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">豆棚閒話</span></span>, early Qing baihua novel</li>
<li><i>Guzhang jue chen</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">鼓掌絕塵</span></span>, late Ming novel collection</li>
<li><i>Bo zhong lian</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">缽中蓮</span></span></li></ul>
<p>These works contain a small handful of unique grammatical features, some of which are not found in contemporary Mandarin, classical Chinese, or in contemporary Wu varieties. They do contain many of the unique features present in contemporary Wu such as pronouns, but clearly indicate that not all of the earlier unique features of these Wu dialects were carried into the present. These works also possess a number of characters uniquely formed to express features not found in the classical language and used some common characters as phonetic loans (see <a href="/wiki/Chinese_character_classification" title="Chinese character classification">Chinese character classification</a>) to express other uniquely Wu vocabulary.
</p><p>During the Ming dynasty, Wu speakers moved into <a href="/wiki/Jianghuai_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Jianghuai Mandarin">Jianghuai Mandarin</a> speaking regions, influencing the Tairu and Tongtai dialects of Jianghuai.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2002541_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2002541-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> During the time between the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> and early Republican era, the main characteristics of modern Wu were formed. The <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhou dialect</a> became the most influential, and many dialectologists use it in citing examples of Wu.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The "Middle Period" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh">中期</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">zhōngqī</span></i>) took place in the middle of the Qing dynasty in the 18th century. Representative works from this section include the operas (especially kunqu operas) by Qian Decang (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">錢德蒼</span></span>) in the collection <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">綴白裘</span></span>, and the legends written by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Shen_Qifeng&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Shen Qifeng (page does not exist)">Shen Qifeng</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%B2%88%E8%B5%B7%E9%B3%B3" class="extiw" title="zh:沈起鳳">zh</a>&#93;</span> or what are known as "<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">沈氏四種</span></span>", as well as huge numbers of tanci (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">彈詞</span></span>) ballads. Many of the common phenomena found in the Shan Ge are not present in works from this period, but we see the production of many new words and new means of using words.
</p><p>The "Late Period" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh">晚期</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">wǎnqī</span></i>) lasts from late Qing to Republican China, in the 19th and 20th centuries. The representative works from this period are Wu vernacular novels (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">蘇白小說</span></span> or <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">吳語小說</span></span>) such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sing-song_Girls_of_Shanghai" title="The Sing-song Girls of Shanghai">The Sing-song Girls of Shanghai</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nine-tailed_Turtle" title="The Nine-tailed Turtle">The Nine-tailed Turtle</a></i>. Other works include:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Haitian Hongxue Ji</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">海天鴻雪記</span></span></li>
<li><i>The Nine-tailed Fox</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">九尾狐</span></span></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Officialdom_Unmasked" title="Officialdom Unmasked">Officialdom Unmasked</a></i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">官場現形記</span></span></li>
<li><i>Wuge Jiaji</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">吳哥甲集</span></span></li>
<li><i>He Dian</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">何典</span></span></li></ul>
<p>Wu-speaking writers who wrote in vernacular Mandarin often left traces of their native varieties in their works, as can be found in <i>Guanchang Xianxing Ji</i> and <i>Fubao Zatan</i> (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">负曝闲谈</span></span>).
</p><p>Another source from this period is from the work of the missionary <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Edkins" title="Joseph Edkins">Joseph Edkins</a>, who gathered large amounts of data and published several educational works on Shanghainese as well as a Bible in Shanghainese and a few other major Wu varieties.
</p><p>Works in this period also saw an explosion of new vocabulary in Wu dialects to describe their changing world. This clearly reflects the great social changes which were occurring during the time.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are currently three works available on the topic:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">明清吴语和现代方言研究</span></span> (<i>Ming and Qing Wu and Modern Dialect Research</i>) by Shi Rujie (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">石汝杰</span></span>)</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">明清文学中的吴语词研究</span></span> (<i>Studies of Wu words found in Ming and Qing literature</i>) by Chu Bannong (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">褚半农</span></span>)</li>
<li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">明清吴语词典</span></span> (<i>Dictionary of Ming and Qing Wu</i>) edited by Shi Rujie (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">石汝杰</span></span>)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-1949">Post-1949</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Post-1949"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%E8%AA%AA%E5%A5%BD%E6%99%AE%E9%80%9A%E8%A9%B1.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/%E8%AA%AA%E5%A5%BD%E6%99%AE%E9%80%9A%E8%A9%B1.png/220px-%E8%AA%AA%E5%A5%BD%E6%99%AE%E9%80%9A%E8%A9%B1.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/%E8%AA%AA%E5%A5%BD%E6%99%AE%E9%80%9A%E8%A9%B1.png/330px-%E8%AA%AA%E5%A5%BD%E6%99%AE%E9%80%9A%E8%A9%B1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/%E8%AA%AA%E5%A5%BD%E6%99%AE%E9%80%9A%E8%A9%B1.png/440px-%E8%AA%AA%E5%A5%BD%E6%99%AE%E9%80%9A%E8%A9%B1.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="530" /></a><figcaption>A sign in <a href="/wiki/Lishui" title="Lishui">Lishui</a> urging people to speak Mandarin: "Speak Mandarin well — It's easier for all of us."</figcaption></figure>
<p>After the founding of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, the strong promotion of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> in the Wu-speaking region yet again influenced the development of Wu Chinese. Wu was gradually excluded from most modern media and schools. Public organizations were required to use Mandarin. With the influx of a migrant non-Wu-speaking population, the near total conversion of public media and organizations to the exclusive use of Mandarin as well as radical Mandarin promotion measures, the modernization and standardization of or literacy in Wu languages became improbable and left them more prone to Mandarinization. The promotion measures, which at present mostly consist of signs like the one pictured, are primarily aimed at limiting the usage of local dialects in conducting public or administrative affairs, although it, like the smoking ban, is commonly violated and it is not so uncommon to hear people speaking local dialects in a government office or a bank. The usage of local dialects in all other spheres is officially tolerated. Standardization of dialects, however, may be perceived as a precursor to possible regionalism, so this, too, would most likely be deterred. On the other hand, few speakers consider their dialect important enough to be written or standardized. To most speakers, dialects are in essence a wholly oral phenomenon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>It is not uncommon to encounter children who grew up with a regional variant of Mandarin as their parent tongue with little or no fluency in a Wu variety at all.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> However, this is primarily when parents are speakers of different languages and communicate in Mandarin and more rarely due to the parents' attitudes towards using language or dialect, which most associate with the warmth of home and family life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Many people<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> have noticed this trend and thus call for the preservation and documentation of not only Wu but all Chinese varieties. The first major attempt was the <i><a href="/wiki/Linguistic_Atlas_of_Chinese_Dialects" title="Linguistic Atlas of Chinese Dialects">Linguistic Atlas of Chinese Dialects</a></i>, which surveyed 2,791 locations across the nation, including 121 Wu locations (a step up from the two locations in PKU's earlier surveys), and led to the formation of an elaborate database including digital recordings of all locations;<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> however, this database is not available to the general public. The atlas's editor, Cao Zhiyun, considers many of these languages "endangered" and has introduced the term <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">濒危方言</span></span> ('languages in danger') or 'endangered dialects' into the Chinese language to raise people's attention to the issue,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> while others<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2016)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> try to draw attention to how the dialects fall under the scope of UNESCO's <a href="/wiki/Intangible_cultural_heritage" title="Intangible cultural heritage">intangible cultural heritage</a> and as such deserve to be preserved and respected.
</p><p>More TV programs are appearing in Wu varieties<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:AUDIENCE" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:AUDIENCE"><span title="An editor has requested that an example be provided. (December 2018)">example  needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and nearly every city/town has at least one show in their native variety. However, they are no longer permitted to air during primetime.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> They are generally more playful than serious and the majority of these shows, such as Hangzhou's <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">阿六头说新闻</span></span> "Old Liutou tells you the news", provide local or regional news in the dialect, but most are limited to fifteen minutes of airtime. Popular video sites such as <a href="/wiki/Youku" title="Youku">Youku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tudou" title="Tudou">Tudou</a> also host a variety of user-uploaded audio and visual media in many Wu languages and dialects, most of which are dialectal TV shows, although some are user-created songs and the like. A number of popular books are also appearing to teach people how to speak the Shanghainese, Suzhou dialect and Wenzhounese<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:AUDIENCE" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:AUDIENCE"><span title="An editor has requested that an example be provided. (December 2018)">example  needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> but they are more playful and entertaining than serious attempts at promoting literacy or standardization.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jianghuai_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Jianghuai Mandarin">Jianghuai Mandarin</a> has replaced Wu as the language of multiple counties in Jiangsu. An example of this is Zaicheng Town in <a href="/wiki/Lishui_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Lishui District">Lishui County</a>; both Jianghuai and Wu languages were spoken in several towns in Lishui, with Wu being spoken by more people in more towns than Jianghuai. The Wu dialect is called "old Zaicheng Speech", while the Jianghuai dialect is called "new Zaicheng speech", with Wu languages being driven rapidly to extinction. Only <span class="clarify-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">old people</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Clarify" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="This passage needs to be better explained. (January 2016)">clarify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> use it to talk to relatives.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The tone or style of material in the vicinity of this tag may not be appropriate for Wikipedia. (January 2016)">tone</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Jianghuai dialect has been present there for about a century, even though all of the surrounding are Wu speaking. Jianghuai was always confined inside the town itself until the 1960s; at present, it is overtaking Wu.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_speakers">Number of speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Number of speakers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wu Chinese was once historically dominant north of the Yangtze River and most of what is now Anhui province during the Sui dynasty. Its strength in areas north of the Yangtze vastly declined from the late Tang dynasty until the late Ming dynasty, when the first characteristics of Early Modern Wu were formed. During the early Qing period, Wu speakers represented about 20% of the whole Chinese population. This percentage drastically declined after the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> devastated the Wu-speaking region, and it was reduced to about 8% by 1984, when the total number of speakers was estimated to be 80 million.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Wu's place within the greater scope of Sinitic varieties is less easily typified than prototypically northern Chinese varieties such as Mandarin or prototypically southern Chinese varieties such as Cantonese. Its original classification, along with the other Sinitic varieties, was established in 1937 by <a href="/wiki/Li_Fang-Kuei" title="Li Fang-Kuei">Li Fang-Kuei</a>, whose boundaries more or less have remained the same<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988180_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988180-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and were adopted by <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Jiahua" title="Yuan Jiahua">Yuan Jiahua</a> in his influential 1961 dialect primer.<sup id="cite_ref-Yuan_Jiahua_hyfygy_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yuan_Jiahua_hyfygy-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sole basis of Li's classification was the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> voiced stops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988180_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988180-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the original sense, a Wu variety was by definition one which retained voiced initials. This definition is problematic considering the devoicing process which has begun in many southern Wu varieties that are surrounded by dialects which retain the ancestral voicing. The loss of voicing in a dialect does not entail that its other features will suddenly become dramatically different from the dialects it has had long historic ties with. It furthermore would place <a href="/wiki/Old_Xiang" title="Old Xiang">Old Xiang</a> in this category. Therefore, more elaborate systems have developed, but they still mostly delineate the same regions. Regardless of the justification, the Wu region has been clearly outlined, and Li's boundary in some ways has remained the de facto standard.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Norman_(sinologist)" title="Jerry Norman (sinologist)">Jerry Norman</a>'s usage, Wu dialects can be considered "central dialects" or dialects that are clearly in a transition zone containing features that typify both northern and southern Chinese varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988197–198_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988197–198-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Possible_Kra.E2.80.93Dai_substratum"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_Kra–Dai_substratum">Possible Kra–Dai substratum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Possible Kra–Dai substratum"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Li Hui (2001) finds possible 126 <a href="/wiki/Kra%E2%80%93Dai_languages" title="Kra–Dai languages">Kra-Dai</a> cognates in the <a href="/wiki/Minhang_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Minhang District">Maqiao</a> Wu dialect spoken in the suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, out of more than 1,000 lexical items surveyed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi200115_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi200115-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> According to the author, these cognates are likely traces of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Yue_language" title="Old Yue language">Old Yue language</a> (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">古越語</span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi200115_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi200115-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The two tables below show lexical comparisons between the Maqiao Wu dialect and Kra-Dai languages as quoted from Li Hui (2001). He notes that, in Wu Chinese, final consonants such as -m, -ɯ, -i, ụ, etc. do not exist, and therefore, -m in the Maqiao dialect tends to become -ŋ, -n, or zero. In some cases, -m even becomes a final glottal stop.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi200119_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi200119-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
