codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8d/WIKITONGUES-_Ivy_speaking_Shanghainese.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Ivy_speaking_Shanghainese.webm.360p.webm#t=00:00:03" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AWIKITONGUES-_Ivy_speaking_Shanghainese.webm&amp;lang=en&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" kind="subtitles" type="text/vtt" srclang="en" label="English ‪(en)‬" data-dir="ltr" /></video></span><figcaption>A video in <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, a Northern Wu language.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Wu languages are spoken in most of <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> province, the whole municipality of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> province, as well as smaller parts of <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> provinces.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Many are located in the lower <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> valley.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dialectologists traditionally establish linguistic boundaries based on several overlapping <a href="/wiki/Isogloss" title="Isogloss">isoglosses</a> of linguistic features. One of the critical historical factors for these boundaries lies in the movement of the population of speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-王文胜_2008_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-王文胜_2008-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> This is often determined by the administrative boundaries established during imperial times. As such, imperial boundaries are essential for delineating one variety from another, and many varieties' isogloss clusters line up perfectly with the county boundaries established in imperial times, although some counties contain more than one variety and others may span several counties.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Another factor that influences movement and transportation as well as the establishment of administrative boundaries is geography.<sup id="cite_ref-王文胜_2008_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-王文胜_2008-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Northernmost Zhejiang and Jiangsu are very flat, in the middle of a river delta, and as such are more uniform than the more mountainous regions farther south towards Fujian. The Taihu varieties, like Mandarin in the flat northern plains, are more homogeneous than Southern Wu, which has a significantly greater diversity of linguistic forms, and this is likely a direct result of geography. Coastal varieties also share more featural affinities, likely because the East China Sea provides a means of transportation. The same phenomenon can be seen with <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min varieties</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_groupings">Major groupings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Major groupings"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wu is divided into two major groups: Northern Wu (<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Běibù Wúyǔ</span></i>) and Southern Wu (<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Nánbù Wúyǔ</span></i>), which are only partially mutually intelligible. Individual words spoken in isolation may be comprehensible among these speakers, but the flowing discourse of everyday life mostly is not. There is another lesser group, Western Wu, synonymous with the Xuanzhou division, which has a larger influence from the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin varieties</a> than Northern Wu, making it typologically much different from the rest of Wu.
</p><p>Southern Wu is well known among <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sinology" title="Sinology">sinologists</a> as being one of the most internally diverse among the <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">Sinitic groups</a>, with very little mutual intelligibility between varieties across subgroups. On the other hand, some Wu varieties like <a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Wenzhounese</a> have gained notoriety for their high incomprehensibility to both Wu and non-Wu speakers alike, so much so that Wenzhounese was used during the Second World War to avoid Japanese interception.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Wu_Dialects.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Wu_Dialects.png/440px-Wu_Dialects.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="369" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/Wu_Dialects.png 1.5x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="503" /></a><figcaption>Map of the main subgroups of Wu in China</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">Language Atlas of China</a></i> (1987), Wu was divided into six subgroups:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Taihu_Wu" title="Taihu Wu">Taihu</a></b> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tai" title="Lake Tai">Lake Tai</a> region): Spoken over much of southern <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> province, including <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wuxi" title="Wuxi">Wuxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changzhou" title="Changzhou">Changzhou</a>, the southern part of <a href="/wiki/Nantong" title="Nantong">Nantong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jingjiang" title="Jingjiang">Jingjiang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Danyang_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Danyang City">Danyang</a>; the city of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>; and the northern part of <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> province, including <a href="/wiki/Ningbo" title="Ningbo">Ningbo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huzhou" title="Huzhou">Huzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaoxing" title="Shaoxing">Shaoxing</a> and Jiaxing. This group makes up the largest population among all Wu speakers. The local varieties of this region are mostly mutually intelligible among each other.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhou dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ningbo_dialect" title="Ningbo dialect">Ningbo dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hangzhou_dialect" title="Hangzhou dialect">Hangzhou dialect</a></li>
<li>Huzhou dialect</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wuxi_dialect" title="Wuxi dialect">Wuxi dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Changzhou_dialect" title="Changzhou dialect">Changzhou dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jiangyin_dialect" title="Jiangyin dialect">Jiangyin dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qihai_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Qihai dialect">Qi–Hai dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jinxiang_dialect" title="Jinxiang dialect">Jinxiang dialect</a></li></ul></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Taizhou_Wu_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="Taizhou Wu dialects">Taizhou</a></b> （<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">台州</span></span>）: Spoken in and around <a href="/wiki/Taizhou,_Zhejiang" title="Taizhou, Zhejiang">Taizhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> province. Taizhou Wu is among the southern varieties that are the closest to Taihu Wu, also known as North Wu, and speakers can communicate with speakers of Taihu Wu.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Taizhou_dialect" title="Taizhou dialect">Taizhou dialect</a></li></ul></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Oujiang</a></b>/Dong'ou (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">東甌</span></span>／<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">东瓯</span></span>): Spoken in and around the city of <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou" title="Wenzhou">Wenzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> province. This variety is the most distinctive and mutually unintelligible amongst all the Wu varieties. Some dialectologists even treat it as a variety separate from the rest of Wu and call it "Ou language" or <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">瓯语</span></span> <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Ōuyǔ</i></span>.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Wenzhounese</a></li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yueqing_dialect&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Yueqing dialect (page does not exist)">Yueqing dialect</a></li></ul></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wuzhou_Wu_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="Wuzhou Wu dialects">Wuzhou</a></b> (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">婺州</span></span>): Spoken in and around <a href="/wiki/Jinhua" title="Jinhua">Jinhua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> province. Like Taizhou Wu, it is somewhat mutually intelligible with Taihu Wu.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Chuqu_Wu_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="Chuqu Wu dialects">Chu–Qu</a></b> （<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">處衢</span></span>）: Spoken in and around <a href="/wiki/Lishui,_Zhejiang" class="mw-redirect" title="Lishui, Zhejiang">Lishui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quzhou" title="Quzhou">Quzhou</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Shangrao_County" class="mw-redirect" title="Shangrao County">Shangrao County</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yushan_County" title="Yushan County">Yushan County</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> province.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Quzhou_dialect" title="Quzhou dialect">Quzhou dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jiangshan_dialect" title="Jiangshan dialect">Jiangshan dialect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qingtian_dialect" title="Qingtian dialect">Qingtian dialect</a></li></ul></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Xuanzhou_Wu_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="Xuanzhou Wu dialects">Xuanzhou</a></b> （<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">宣州</span></span>）: Spoken in and around <a href="/wiki/Xuancheng" title="Xuancheng">Xuancheng</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> province. This part of Wu is becoming less spoken since the campaign started by the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a>, and it is being slowly replaced by the immigrant Mandarin from north of the Yangtse river.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Wu">Southern Wu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Southern Wu"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chinese dialectologist <a href="/wiki/Cao_Zhiyun" title="Cao Zhiyun">Cao Zhiyun</a> has rearranged some of the divisions based on a larger corpus of data.
According to Cao, Southern Wu can be divided into three broad divisions (note that he is using the pre-republican boundaries for the cited locations):<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Jin–Qu</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">金衢</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Jīn–Qú</span></i>), which contains twelve locations.
<ul><li>Jinhua Prefecture: <a href="/wiki/Jinhua" title="Jinhua">Jinhua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tangxi,_Jinhua" title="Tangxi, Jinhua">Tangxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanxi,_Zhejiang" title="Lanxi, Zhejiang">Lanxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pujiang_County,_Zhejiang" title="Pujiang County, Zhejiang">Pujiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yiwu" title="Yiwu">Yiwu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dongyang" title="Dongyang">Dongyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pan%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Pan&#39;an">Pan'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yongkang,_Zhejiang" title="Yongkang, Zhejiang">Yongkang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wuyi_County,_Zhejiang" title="Wuyi County, Zhejiang">Wuyi</a></li>
<li>Quzhou Prefecture: <a href="/wiki/Quzhou" title="Quzhou">Quzhou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longyou" class="mw-redirect" title="Longyou">Longyou</a></li>
<li>Lishui Prefecture: <a href="/wiki/Jinyun" class="mw-redirect" title="Jinyun">Jinyun</a></li></ul></li>
<li><b>Shang–Li</b> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">上丽</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">上麗</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Shàng–Lí</span></i>), which contains seventeen locations and has two subdivisions:
<ul><li><i>Shang–Shan</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">上山</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Shàng–Shān</span></i>), which contains six locations.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Shangrao" title="Shangrao">Shangrao</a> prefecture, Jiangxi province: <a href="/wiki/Shangrao" title="Shangrao">Shangrao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangfeng" class="mw-redirect" title="Guangfeng">Guangfeng</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yushan_County" title="Yushan County">Yushan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Quzhou" title="Quzhou">Quzhou</a> prefecture: <a href="/wiki/Kaihua" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaihua">Kaihua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changshan" title="Changshan">Changshan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangshan" title="Jiangshan">Jiangshan</a></li></ul></li>
<li><i>Lishui</i> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">丽水</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant"><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">麗水</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn">Líshuǐ</span></i>), which contains eleven locations.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lishui" title="Lishui">Lishui</a> Prefecture: <a href="/wiki/Lishui" title="Lishui">Lishui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suichang" class="mw-redirect" title="Suichang">Suichang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songyang" class="mw-redirect" title="Songyang">Songyang</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Xuanping_County,_Zhejiang&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Xuanping County, Zhejiang (page does not exist)">Xuanping</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%AE%A3%E5%B9%B3%E5%8E%BF_(%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F)" class="extiw" title="zh:宣平县 (浙江)">zh</a>&#93;</span> (former county of Lishui, now belonging to <a href="/wiki/Wuyi_County,_Zhejiang" title="Wuyi County, Zhejiang">Wuyi</a>), <a href="/wiki/Qingtian" class="mw-redirect" title="Qingtian">Qingtian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yunhe_County" title="Yunhe County">Yunhe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jingning_She_Autonomous_County" title="Jingning She Autonomous County">Jingning She Autonomous County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Longquan" title="Longquan">Longquan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qingyuan_County,_Zhejiang" title="Qingyuan County, Zhejiang">Qingyuan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wenzhou" title="Wenzhou">Wenzhou</a> Prefecture: <a href="/wiki/Taishun" class="mw-redirect" title="Taishun">Taishun</a> county</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nanping" title="Nanping">Nanping</a> Prefecture in Fujian: <a href="/wiki/Pucheng_County,_Fujian" title="Pucheng County, Fujian">Pucheng</a></li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Oujiang</a></b> or Ou River, which contains eight locations.
<ul><li>Wenzhou prefecture: <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou" title="Wenzhou">Wenzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yongjia_County" title="Yongjia County">Yongjia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yueqing" title="Yueqing">Yueqing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rui%27an" title="Rui&#39;an">Rui'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dongtou" class="mw-redirect" title="Dongtou">Dongtou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pingyang_County" title="Pingyang County">Pingyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cangnan" class="mw-redirect" title="Cangnan">Cangnan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wencheng_County" title="Wencheng County">Wencheng</a> (excluding the Min speaking regions of Pingyang and Cangnan).</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>This was later adopted by the second edition of Li's Language Atlas.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Wu dialects are notable among Chinese varieties in having kept the "muddy" (<a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a>; whispery voiced word-initially) <a href="/wiki/Plosive" title="Plosive">plosives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Fricative consonant">fricatives</a> of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>, such as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b/,<span class="wrap"> </span>/d/,<span class="wrap"> </span>/ɡ/,<span class="wrap"> </span>/z/,<span class="wrap"> </span>/v/,</span> <i>etc.</i>, thus maintaining the three-way contrast of Middle Chinese <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Stop consonant">stop consonants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Affricates" class="mw-redirect" title="Affricates">affricates</a>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p<span class="wrap"> </span>pʰ<span class="wrap"> </span>b/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tɕ<span class="wrap"> </span>tɕʰ<span class="wrap"> </span>dʑ/</span>, <i>etc.</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYan200687_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYan200687-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> (For example, 「<span lang="zh">凍</span> <span lang="zh">痛</span> <span lang="zh">洞</span>」 <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t<span class="wrap"> </span>tʰ<span class="wrap"> </span>d/</span>, where other varieties have only <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t<span class="wrap"> </span>tʰ/</span>.) Because Wu dialects never lost these voiced obstruents, the tone split of Middle Chinese may still be allophonic, and most dialects have three syllabic <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tones</a> (though counted as eight in traditional descriptions). In <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Urban Shanghainese</a>, some more tones have undergone merging, and thus can be analysed to only have two phonemic tones.
</p><p>Wu varieties and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> have the largest <a href="/wiki/Vowel#Systems" title="Vowel">vowel quality inventories</a> in the world.  The <a href="/wiki/Jinhui_dialect" title="Jinhui dialect">Jinhui dialect</a> spoken in Shanghai's <a href="/wiki/Fengxian_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Fengxian District">Fengxian District</a> has 20 vowel qualities.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Because of these different changes within Wu, which gives it its unique quality, it has also sometimes been called the "<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> of China".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>For more details, see <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese#Phonology" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese §&#160;Phonology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect#Phonology" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhou dialect §&#160;Phonology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese#Phonology" title="Wenzhounese">Wenzhounese §&#160;Phonology</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronoun</a> systems of many Wu dialects are complex when it comes to personal and demonstrative pronouns. For example, Wu exhibits <a href="/wiki/Clusivity" title="Clusivity">clusivity</a> (having different forms of the first-person plural pronoun depending on whether or not the addressee is included). Wu employs six demonstratives, three of which are used to refer to close objects, and three of which are used for farther objects.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In terms of <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a>, Wu uses SVO (like <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a>), but unlike Mandarin, it also has a high occurrence of SOV and in some cases OSV.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue200394_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue200394-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In terms of phonology, tone <a href="/wiki/Sandhi" title="Sandhi">sandhi</a> is extremely complex, and helps parse multisyllabic words and idiomatic phrases. In some cases, indirect objects are distinguished from direct objects by a voiced/voiceless distinction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In most cases, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classifier" title="Chinese classifier">classifiers</a> take the place of genitive particles and articles – a quality shared with <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> – as shown by the following examples<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Wu</th>
<th>Wu translation</th>
<th>Mandarin</th>
<th>Mandarin translation
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">本書交關好看</span></span></td>
<td>the volume [of] book is very good</td>
<td><span title="Mandarin Chinese-language text"><span lang="cmn">書很好看</span></span></td>
<td>the book is very good
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">我支筆</span></span></td>
<td>my stick [of] pen</td>
<td><span title="Mandarin Chinese-language text"><span lang="cmn">我的筆</span></span></td>
<td>my pen
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">渠碗粥</span></span></td>
<td>his bowl [of] congee</td>
<td><span title="Mandarin Chinese-language text"><span lang="cmn">他的粥</span></span></td>
<td>his congee
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plural_pronouns">Plural pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Plural pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wu dialects vary in the way they pluralize pronouns. In the <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhou dialect</a>, second- and third-person pronouns are suffixed with <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[toʔ]</span>, while the first-person plural is a separate root, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ni]</span>, from the singular.  In <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, the first-person pronoun is suffixed with <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">伲</span></span>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and third-person with <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[la˦]</span> (underlying <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/la˥˧/</span>), but the second-person plural is a separate root, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[nʌ˨˧]</span>.  In the <a href="/wiki/Haiyan_County,_Zhejiang" title="Haiyan County, Zhejiang">Haiyan</a> dialect, first- and third-person pronouns are pluralized with <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[la]</span>, but the second-person plural is a separate root <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[na]</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue200386_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue200386-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifiers">Classifiers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Classifiers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All nouns could have just one <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classifier" title="Chinese classifier">classifier</a> in Shanghainese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue200385_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue200385-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanization">Romanization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Romanization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">Romanization of Wu Chinese</a></div>
<p>There are three major schools of <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">romanization of Wu Chinese</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Vocabulary"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like other varieties of Southern Chinese, Wu Chinese retains some archaic vocabulary from <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a>. For instance, for "to speak" or "speaking", Wu dialects, with the exception of Hangzhou dialect, use <i>góng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese">Simplified Chinese</a>: <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">讲</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese">Traditional Chinese</a>: <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">講</span></span>), whereas Mandarin uses <i>shuō</i> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese">Simplified Chinese</a>: <span title="Mandarin Chinese-language text"><span lang="cmn">说</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese">Traditional Chinese</a>: <span title="Mandarin Chinese-language text"><span lang="cmn">說</span></span>). Furthermore, in <a href="/wiki/Guangfeng" class="mw-redirect" title="Guangfeng">Guangfeng</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yushan_County" title="Yushan County">Yushan counties</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> province, <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">曰</span></span> (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[je]</span> or <i>yuē</i>) is generally preferred over its Mandarin counterpart. In <a href="/wiki/Shangrao" title="Shangrao">Shangrao</a> county of <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a> <a href="/wiki/Province" title="Province">province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese">Simplified Chinese</a>: <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">话</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese">Traditional Chinese</a>: <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">話</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i>huà</i>/<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[wa]</span> is preferred over the spoken Mandarin version of the word for "to speak" or "speaking". For Wu dialects closer to Fuzhou such as <a href="/wiki/Ningbo_dialect" title="Ningbo dialect">Ningbonese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wuzhou_Wu" title="Wuzhou Wu">Jinhuanese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Wenzhounese</a> the word <i>nóng</i> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese">Simplified Chinese</a>: <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">侬</span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese">Traditional Chinese</a>: <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">儂</span></span>) is used for "person" instead of <i>rén</i>/<i>nin</i> <span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">人</span></span>, similar to <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a>.
</p>

<p>In Wu dialects, the morphology of the words are similar, but the characters are switched around. Not all Wu Chinese words exhibit this phenomenon, only some words in some dialects.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colloquialisms">Colloquialisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Colloquialisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Wu Chinese, there are colloquialisms that are traced back to ancestral Chinese varieties, such as Middle or Old Chinese. Many of those colloquialisms are cognates of other words found in other modern southern Chinese dialects, such as <a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">Xiang</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a>.
</p><p>Mandarin equivalents and their pronunciation on Wu Chinese are in parentheses. All IPA transcriptions and examples<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> listed below are from <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>.
</p>
<ul><li>「<span lang="zh">鑊子</span>」 (<span lang="zh">鍋子</span>) <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɦɔʔ<span class="wrap"> </span>tsɨ]<span class="wrap"> </span>(ku<span class="wrap"> </span>tsɨ)</span> <a href="/wiki/Wok" title="Wok">wok</a>, cooking pot. The Mandarin equivalent term is also used, but both of them are synonyms and are thus interchangeable.</li>
<li>「<span lang="zh">衣裳</span>」 (<span lang="zh">衣服</span>) <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i<span class="wrap"> </span>zã]<span class="wrap"> </span>(i<span class="wrap"> </span>voʔ)</span> clothing. Found in other Chinese dialects. It is a reference to traditional <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese_clothing" class="mw-redirect" title="Han Chinese clothing">Han Chinese clothing</a>, where it consists of the upper garments 「<span lang="zh">衣</span>」 and the lower garments 「<span lang="zh">裳</span>」.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Literature"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The genres of <i><a href="/wiki/Kunqu" title="Kunqu">kunqu</a></i> opera and <i><a href="/wiki/Tanci" title="Tanci">tanci</a></i> song, appearing in the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>, were the first instances of the use of Wu dialect in literature. By the turn of the 20th century it was used in several novels that had prostitution as a subject.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp33_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp33-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In many of these novels, Wu is mainly used as dialogue of prostitute characters. In one work, <i><a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Flowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Shanghai Flowers">Shanghai Flowers</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Han_Bangqing" title="Han Bangqing">Han Bangqing</a>, all of the dialogue is in Wu.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Wu originally developed in genres related to oral performance. It was used in manners related to oral performance when it proliferated in written literature and it was widely used in fiction about prostitutes, a particular genre, and not in other genres. Donald B. Snow, author of <i>Cantonese as Written Language: The Growth of a Written Chinese Vernacular</i>, compared the development of Wu in this manner to the patterns of <a href="/wiki/Written_vernacular_Chinese" title="Written vernacular Chinese">Baihua</a> and Japanese vernacular writing.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Jean Duval, author of "The Nine-Tailed Turtle: Pornography or 'fiction of exposure", at the time <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nine-tailed_Turtle" title="The Nine-tailed Turtle">The Nine-tailed Turtle</a></i> by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Zhang_Chunfan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Zhang Chunfan (page does not exist)">Zhang Chunfan</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%BC%B5%E6%98%A5%E5%B8%86_(%E6%B8%85%E6%9C%9D)" class="extiw" title="zh:張春帆 (清朝)">zh</a>&#93;</span> (<span lang="zh-Hant">張春帆</span>) was published, it was one of the most popular novels written in the Wu dialect.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnificent_Dreams_in_Shanghai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Magnificent Dreams in Shanghai (page does not exist)">Magnificent Dreams in Shanghai</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hant">海上繁華夢</span>) by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sun_Jiazhen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sun Jiazhen (page does not exist)">Sun Jiazhen</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">孫家振</span>) was another example of a prostitute novel with Wu dialogue from the turn of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp3334_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp3334-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Snow wrote that Wu literature "achieved a certain degree of prominence" by 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> After 1910 there had been no novels which were as popular as <i>The Nine-tailed Turtle</i> or the critical acclaim garnered by <i>Shanghai Flowers</i>. In the popular fiction of the early 20th century the usage of Wu remained in use in prostitute dialogue but, as asserted by Snow, "apparently" did not extend beyond that.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In 1926 <a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">Hu Shih</a> stated that of all of the Chinese dialects, within literature, Wu had the brightest future.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Snow concluded that instead Wu dialect writing became "a transient phenomenon that died out not long after its growth gathered steam."<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Snow argued that the primary reason was the increase of prestige and importance in Baihua, and that one other contributing reason was changing market factors since Shanghai's publishing industry, which grew, served all of China and not just Shanghai.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Duval argued that many Chinese critics had a low opinion of Wu works, mainly originating from the eroticism within them, and that contributed to the decline in Wu literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Snowp34_60-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snowp34-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFXiong2006" class="citation book cs1">Xiong, Victor Cunrui (2006). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=UOcU_7H0G-UC&amp;q=yangdi+wu+dialect&amp;pg=PA19"><i>Emperor Yang of the Sui Dynasty: His Life, Times, and Legacy</i></a> (illustrated, annotated&#160;ed.). Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 March</span> 2012</span>. <q>Yangdi also conversed fluently with his wife in the Wu dialect of the South. For a Northerner, a high level of competence in this dialect was no mean feat: It required years of early exposure. Yangdi probably picked it up at an early age from Lady Xiao, whose grandfather Xiao Cha (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">蕭詧</span></span>) grew up at the court of Liang Wudi (<span title="Wu Chinese-language text"><span lang="wuu">梁武帝</span></span>) in Jiankang, a Wu dialect area, before setting up his own court in Jiangling.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Emperor+Yang+of+the+Sui+Dynasty%3A+His+Life%2C+Times%2C+and+Legacy&amp;rft.pages=19&amp;rft.edition=illustrated%2C+annotated&amp;rft.pub=SUNY+Press&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7914-6587-5&amp;rft.aulast=Xiong&amp;rft.aufirst=Victor+Cunrui&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DUOcU_7H0G-UC%26q%3Dyangdi%2Bwu%2Bdialect%26pg%3DPA19&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWu+Chinese" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFWalravenRemco_E._Breuker2007" class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-long-vol">Walraven, Boudewijn; Remco E. (ed.). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=yCDZtFu_1UIC&amp;pg=PA341"><i>Korea in the Middle: Korean Studies and Area Studies&#160;: Essays in Honour of Boudewijn Walraven</i></a>. Vol.&#160;153 of CNWS publications (illustrated&#160;ed.). CNWS Publications. p.&#160;341. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-90-5789-153-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-90-5789-153-3"><bdi>978-90-5789-153-3</bdi></a>. <q>A prosimetrical rendition, entitled Xue Rengui kuahai zheng Liao gushi <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">薛仁貴跨海征遼故事</span></span> (The story of Xue Rengui crossing the sea and Pacifying Liao), which shares its opening prose paragraph with the Xue Rengui zheng Liao shilüe, is preserved in a printing of 1471; it is one of the shuochang cihua <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">說晿詞話</span></span> (ballad-narratives)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Korea+in+the+Middle%3A+Korean+Studies+and+Area+Studies+%3A+Essays+in+Honour+of+Boudewijn+Walraven&amp;rft.pages=341&amp;rft.edition=illustrated&amp;rft.pub=CNWS+Publications&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-90-5789-153-3&amp;rft.aulast=Walraven&amp;rft.aufirst=Boudewijn&amp;rft.au=Remco+E.+Breuker&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DyCDZtFu_1UIC%26pg%3DPA341&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWu+Chinese" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resources_on_Wu_dialects">Resources on Wu dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Resources on Wu dialects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://wugniu.com/">Wugniu.com</a>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://wugniu.com/dict">Wu Dictionary</a>– Wu dictionary available in 8 dialects.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://wugniu.com/">Wu Character Pronunciation</a> Shows how character(s) are pronounced in Wu, data available for many dialects.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://wugniu.com/map">Wu Pronunciation Map</a> How a character is pronounced in Wu depending on the region.</li></ul></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.glossika.com">glossika.com</a>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.glossika.com/en/dict/wu/index.php">Shanghainese Wu Dictionary</a>– Search in <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a>, or <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20121203203408/http://www.glossika.com/en/dict/wu/index.php">Archived</a> 3 December 2012 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.glossika.com/en/dict/classification/wu/index.php">Classification of Wu Dialects</a> – By James Campbell</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.glossika.com/en/dict/tones/wu.htm">Tones in Wu Dialects</a> – Compiled by James Campbell</li></ul></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1 cs1-prop-script"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080503055955/http://sinolect.org/bbs/"><bdi lang="zh">吴语论坛</bdi></a> &#91;Linguistic Forum of Wu Chinese&#93;. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://sinolect.org/bbs/">the original</a> on 3 May 2008.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=%E5%90%B4%E8%AF%AD%E8%AE%BA%E5%9D%9B&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fsinolect.org%2Fbbs%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWu+Chinese" class="Z3988"></span></li></ul>
<p>A BBS set up in 2004, in which topics such as phonology, grammar, orthography and romanization of Wu Chinese are widely talked about.  The cultural and linguistic diversity within China is also a significant concerning of this forum.
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1 cs1-prop-script cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source-2"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100501162846/http://wu-chinese.com/"><bdi lang="zh">吴语协会</bdi></a> &#91;Wu Chinese Online Association&#93; (in Wu). Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://wu-chinese.com">the original</a> on 1 May 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">7 October</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=%E5%90%B4%E8%AF%AD%E5%8D%8F%E4%BC%9A&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwu-chinese.com&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWu+Chinese" class="Z3988"></span></li></ul>
<p>A website aimed at modernization of Wu Chinese, including basics of Wu, Wu romanization scheme, pronunciation dictionaries of different dialects, Wu input method development, Wu research literatures, written Wu experiment, Wu orthography, a discussion forum etc.
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1 cs1-prop-script cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://wu-chinese.com/wu-chinese/"><bdi lang="zh">江南雅音话吴语</bdi></a> &#91;The elegant language in Jiangnan area&#93; (in Chinese (China)).</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=%E6%B1%9F%E5%8D%97%E9%9B%85%E9%9F%B3%E8%AF%9D%E5%90%B4%E8%AF%AD&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwu-chinese.com%2Fwu-chinese%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWu+Chinese" class="Z3988"></span></li></ul>
<p>References on Wu Chinese, including tones of the sub-dialects.
</p>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://tatoeba.org/eng/sentences/show_all_in/wuu/none/none/indifferent">Tatoeba Project Tatoeba.org</a> - Examples sentences in Shanghainese dialect, and in Suzhouan dialect.</li>
<li><a href="http://hdl.handle.net/10125/38799" class="extiw" title="hdl:10125/38799">Wu wordlist</a> available through <a href="/wiki/Kaipuleohone" title="Kaipuleohone">Kaipuleohone</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://wu-chinese.com/minidict/index.php">Pronunciation dictionary</a> - with audio from various Chinese cities.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Articles">Articles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wu_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Articles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20101210075102/http://www.waccglobal.org/en/20061-celebrating-cultural-diversity/564-Globalization-national-culture-and-the-search-for-identity-A-Chinese-dilemma.html">Globalization, National Culture and the Search for Identity: A Chinese Dilemma (1st Quarter of 2006, Media Development)</a> – A comprehensive article, written by Wu Mei and Guo Zhenzhi of World Association for Christian Communication, related to the struggle for national cultural unity by current Chinese Communist national government while desperately fighting for preservation on Chinese regional cultures that have been the precious roots of all Han Chinese people (including Hangzhou Wu dialect). Excellent for anyone doing research on Chinese language linguistic, anthropology on Chinese culture, international business, foreign languages, global studies, and translation/interpretation.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ytlcommunity.com/commnews/shownews.asp?newsid=12482">Modernisation a Threat to Dialects in China</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304051506/http://www.ytlcommunity.com/commnews/shownews.asp?newsid=12482">Archived</a> 4 March 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> – An excellent article originally from Straits Times Interactive through YTL Community website, it provides an insight of Chinese dialects, both major and minor, losing their speakers to Standard Mandarin due to greater mobility and interaction. Excellent for anyone doing research on Chinese language linguistic, anthropology on Chinese culture, international business, foreign languages, global studies, and translation/interpretation.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20050309120244/http://www.middleburycampus.com/news/2002/02/27/NewsSpecialTheTowerOfBabel/Middlebury.Expands.Study.Abroad.Horizons-192768.shtml">Middlebury Expands Study Abroad Horizons</a> – An excellent article including a section on future exchange programs in learning Chinese language in Hangzhou (plus colorful, positive impression on the Hangzhou dialect, too). Requires registration of online account before viewing.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160106175452/http://www.thestandard.com.hk/weekend_news_detail.asp?pp_cat=30&amp;art_id=5935&amp;sid=5491218&amp;con_type=1&amp;d_str=20051119">Mind your language (from The Standard, Hong Kong)</a> – This newspaper article provides a deep insight on the danger of decline in the usage of dialects, including Wu dialects, other than the rising star of Standard Mandarin. It also mentions an exception where some grassroots' organizations and, sometimes, larger institutions, are the force behind the preservation of their dialects. Another excellent article for research on Chinese language linguistics, anthropology on Chinese culture, international business, foreign languages, global studies, and translation/interpretation.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140307125536/http://www.asiamedia.ucla.edu/article-eastasia.asp?parentid=42837">China: Dialect use on TV worries Beijing (originally from Straits Times Interactive, Singapore and posted on AsiaMedia Media News Daily from UCLA)</a> – Article on the use of dialects other than standard Mandarin in China where strict media censorship is high.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070603020022/http://www.radio86.co.uk/explore-learn/lifestyle-in-china/2410/standard-or-local-chinese-tv-programs-in-dialect">Standard or Local Chinese – TV Programs in Dialect (from Radio86.co.uk)</a> – Another article on the use of dialects other than standard Mandarin in China.</li></ul>
